<TABLE>
<TR><TD COLSPAN=5><a name="description"></a>&nbsp;<p><p align='center'><strong> 1.A.8 The Major Intrinsic </strong><strong>Aquaporin </strong><strong>Protein, (MIP) Family</strong></p>
<p>The Major Intrinsic Protein (MIP) of the human lens of the eye (Aqp0), after which the MIP family was named, represents about 60% of the protein in the lens cell.  In the native form, it is an aquaporin, but during lens development, it becomes proteolytically truncated.  The channel, which normally houses 6-9 water molecules, becomes constricted so only three remain, and these are trapped in a closed conformation (<span id='ref3806.15351655' class='cite'>Gonen <em>et al</em>., 2004a</span>,<span id='ref2801.15141214' class='cite'>b</span>).  These truncated tetramers form intercellular adhesive junctions (head to head), yielding a crystalline array that mediates lens formation with cells tightly packed as required to form a clear lens (<span id='ref3807.17156589' class='cite'>Gonen and Walz, 2006</span>). Aquaporin ion conductance properties are defined by the membrane environment, the protein structure, and the cell physiology (<span id='ref34554533.35340612' class='cite'>Henderson <em>et al.</em> 2022</span>). Aqps in some organisms have been the subject of studies such as ticks. Gene expression of AQPs in different tick tissues and stages showed the highest expression levels in salivary glands and gut of adult female <em>Haemaphysalis qinghaiensis</em>. (<span id='ref34554586.35250616' class='cite'>Niu <em>et al.</em> 2022</span>). AQPs are differentially expressed in various cardiovascular tissues of humans and participate in water transmembrane transport, cell migration, metabolism, and inflammatory responses (<span id='ref34554986.35905892' class='cite'>Shangzu <em>et al.</em> 2022</span>). AQP1, AQP2, AQP4, AQP5, and AQP8 are primarily water selective, whereas AQP3, AQP7, AQP9, and AQP10 (called 'aqua-glyceroporins') also transport glycerol and other small solutes. AQPs play roles in cancer cell growth, migration, invasion, and angiogenesis (<span id='ref34554995.35847914' class='cite'>Moon <em>et al.</em> 2022</span>). In the bivalve, the invasive freshwater mussel <em>Dreissena rostriformis,</em> midbody-localized aquaporin mediates intercellular lumen expansion during early cleavage (<span id='ref34555072.35774230' class='cite'>Zieger <em>et al.</em> 2022</span>). Aqp-mediated water distributions' in astrocytes under normal and pathological conditions have been reviewed (<span id='ref34555139.36010640' class='cite'>Zhou <em>et al.</em> 2022</span>). Several methods have been developed for the measurement of water permeability, both in living cells and in tissues (<span id='ref34555839.36717506' class='cite'>Solenov <em>et al.</em> 2023</span>). The importance of aquaporins in fetal developmenthas been reviewed (<span id='ref34555840.36717499' class='cite'>Mart&iacute;nez and Damiano 2023</span>). The biological functions of AQPs are regulated by posttranslational 
modifications, e.g., phosphorylation, ubiquitination, glycosylation, 
subcellular distribution, degradation and protein interactions (<span id='ref34555841.36717484' class='cite'>Xiong <em>et al.</em> 2023</span>). dbAQP-SNP is a database of missense single-nucleotide polymorphisms in human aquaporins (<span id='ref34556327.36913438' class='cite'>Dande and Sankararamakrishnan 2023</span>). <span id='ref34556436.37003962' class='cite'>Raza <em>et al.</em> 2023 </span>unveiled the complete genomic atlas of aquaporins across the genus <em>Oryza</em>. The genome-wide identification and gene expression analysis of sweet cherry aquaporins (<em>Prunus avium</em> L.) under abiotic stresses have been reported (<span id='ref34556519.37107698' class='cite'>Salvatierra <em>et al.</em> 2023</span>). The genome-wide identification of Aqp family members related to spermatogenesis in turbot (<em>Scophthalmus maximus</em>) has been achieved (<span id='ref34556999.37511528' class='cite'>Wang <em>et al.</em> 2023</span>).&nbsp; Plasma membrane aquaporins of the PIP1 and PIP2 subfamilies facilitate hydrogen peroxide diffusion into plant roots (<span id='ref34557070.36471241' class='cite'>Israel <em>et al.</em> 2022</span>). Roles of AQPs in epilepsy and seizure onset in humans have been discussed and reviewed (<span id='ref34557086.37569297' class='cite'>Bonosi <em>et al.</em> 2023</span>).</p>
<p><em></em></p>
<p><em> </em></p>
<p>Lipids crystallize with the protein (<span id='ref3808.16319884' class='cite'>Gonen <em>et al</em>., 2005</span>). Ion channel activity has been shown for Aquaporins 0, 1, and 6, <em>Drosophila</em> Big Brain and plant Nodulin-26 (<span id='ref8346.22342689' class='cite'>Yool and Campbell, 2012</span>).&nbsp; Roles of aquaporins in human cancer have been reviewed (<span id='ref10370.24061477' class='cite'>Pareek <em>et al.</em> 2013</span>) as have their folding pathways (<span id='ref34543758.25462169' class='cite'>Klein <em>et al.</em> 2015</span>). AQPs may act as transmembrane osmosensors in red cells, secretory granules and microorganisms (<span id='ref34543873.25791748' class='cite'>Hill and Shachar-Hill 2015</span>).&nbsp; MIP superfamly proteins and variations of their selectivity filters have been reviewed (<span id='ref34544046.25950979' class='cite'>Verma <em>et al.</em> 2015</span>). Their evolution has been discussed (<span id='ref34546545.28258564' class='cite'>Ishibashi <em>et al.</em> 2017</span>). AQPs have a variety of functions and are related to inner ear diseases such as Meniere's disease, sensorineural hearing loss, and presbycusis (<span id='ref34550337.31593306' class='cite'>Dong <em>et al.</em> 2019</span>). AQPs are also important for male reproductive health (<span id='ref34550561.31816311' class='cite'>Carrageta <em>et al.</em> 2019</span>).&nbsp; The evolution of the aquaporin superfamily has been discussed (<span id='ref34551045.32061337' class='cite'>Ishibashi <em>et al.</em> 2020</span>). The cellular functions of aquaporins are regulated mainly by 
posttranslational modifications, e.g., phosphorylation, ubiquitination, 
glycosylation, subcellular distribution, degradation, and protein 
interactions (<span id='ref34551113.32138947' class='cite'>Li <em>et al.</em> 2020</span>). Aquaporins play roles in inflammation (<span id='ref34551666.32435911' class='cite'>Mariajoseph-Antony <em>et al.</em> 2020</span>) and in various aspects of health and disease (<span id='ref34551776.32564785' class='cite'>Magouliotis <em>et al.</em> 2020</span>). They play major roles in secretion of saliva by salivary glands, and their disruption can cause a variety of diseases (<span id='ref34551841.32630469' class='cite'>D'Agostino <em>et al.</em> 2020</span>). Aquaporins in mamalian lungs have been reviewed (<span id='ref34553503.33096317' class='cite'>Yadav <em>et al.</em> 2020</span>). Lineage-level divergence of copepod glycerol transporters and the emergence of isoform-specific trafficking regulation has been documented (<span id='ref34553636.34059783' class='cite'>Catal&aacute;n-Garc&iacute;a <em>et al.</em> 2021</span>). Aquaporins play key roles in fluid homeostasis, glandular secretions, signal transduction and sensation, barrier function, immunity and inflammation, cell migration, and angiogenesis (<span id='ref34554495.35163313' class='cite'>Wagner <em>et al.</em> 2022</span>). Aquaporins are gated, opening and closing to control water permeation (<span id='ref34555506.36293170' class='cite'>Ozu <em>et al.</em> 2022</span>). They play roles in breast cancer progression and treatment (<span id='ref34555595.36212456' class='cite'>Charlestin <em>et al.</em> 2022</span>). The function of aquaporins in gastrointestinal fluid absorption and secretion have been discussed (<span id='ref34557216.37681902' class='cite'>Calamita and Delporte 2023</span>).</p>
<p>The MIP family is large and diverse, possessing thousands of members that form transmembrane channels.  These channel proteins function in water, small carbohydrate (e.g., glycerol), urea, NH<sub>3</sub>, CO<sub>2</sub>, H<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub> and ion transport by energy-independent mechanisms.  For example, the glycerol channel, FPS1p of <em>Saccharomyces cerevisiae</em> mediates uptake of arsenite and antimonite (<span id='ref1957.11442837' class='cite'>Wysocki <em>et al</em>., 2001</span>).  Ion permeability appears to occur through a pathway different than that used for water/glycerol transport and may involve a channel at the 4 subunit interface rather than the channels through the subunits (<span id='ref2005.11410596' class='cite'>Saparov <em>et al</em>., 2001</span>).  MIP family members are found ubiquitously in bacteria, archaea and eukaryotes.  Phylogenetic clustering of the proteins is largely according to phylum of the organisms of origin, but one or more clusters are observed for each phylogenetic kingdom (plants, animals, yeast, bacteria and archaea) (<span id='ref209.8849412' class='cite'>Park and Saier, 1996</span>). MIPs are classified into five subfamilies in higher plants, including
plasma membrane (PIPs), tonoplast (TIPs), NOD26-like (NIPs), small basic (SIPs) and unclassified X
(XIPs) intrinsic proteins.&nbsp; One of the plant clusters includes only tonoplast (TIP) proteins, while another includes plasma membrane (PIP) proteins (<span id='ref34544552.26397813' class='cite'>de Paula Santos Martins <em>et al.</em> 2015</span>). Aquaporins in <em>Nicotiana tabacum</em> have been tabulated, and their relationships to other Solanaceae species have been described (<span id='ref34551762.32517797' class='cite'>De Rosa <em>et al.</em> 2020</span>). A genome analysis of <em>Betula pendula</em> (silver birch) identified 33 putative genes encoding full-length AQP sequences (BpeAQPs). They are grouped into five subfamilies, representing ten plasma membrane intrinsic proteins (PIPs), eight tonoplast intrinsic proteins (TIPs), eight NOD26-like intrinsic proteins (NIPs), four X intrinsic proteins (XIPs), and three small basic intrinsic proteins (SIPs) (<span id='ref34553615.34298887' class='cite'>Venisse <em>et al.</em> 2021</span>). Fungal X-intrinsic protein aquaporins from <em>Trichoderma atroviride</em> have been studied (<span id='ref34553645.33672420' class='cite'>Amira <em>et al.</em> 2021</span>). In the parasidic helminthes, AQPs play&nbsp; roles in promoting the transport of water, osmoregulation, uptake of nutrients, release of toxic metabolic products and transport of antiparasitic drugs (<span id='ref34552303.33185072' class='cite'>Wang and Ye 2020</span>). Their involvement in diseases pathogenesis has been reviewed (<span id='ref34554212.33559160' class='cite'>Ala <em>et al.</em> 2021</span>). In humans, AQP3, AQP7, AQP9, and AQP10, play critical roles in cancer. Overexpression or knockdown of AQGPs can promote or inhibit cancer cell proliferation, migration, invasion, apoptosis, epithelial-mesenchymal transition and metastasis, and the expression levels of AQGPs are closely linked to the prognosis of cancer patients. The expression patterns of AQPs in different cancers as well as the relationship between the expression patterns and prognosis have been reviewed (<span id='ref34555168.35972604' class='cite'>Wang <em>et al.</em> 2022</span>). The role of AQP5 in the biology of lung adenocarcinoma as well as its prognostic value have been reviewed (<span id='ref34556055.36766810' class='cite'>Jaskiewicz <em>et al.</em> 2023</span>).</p>
<p>The known aquaporins cluster loosely together on a pylogenetic tree as do the known glycerol facilitators.  MIP family proteins are believed to form aqueous pores that selectively allow passive transport of their solute(s) across the membrane with minimal apparent recognition.  Aquaporins selectively transport water (but not glycerol) while glycerol facilitators selectively transport glycerol but not water.  Some aquaporins can transport NH<sub>3</sub> and CO<sub>2</sub>.  Glycerol facilitators function as solute nonspecific channels, and may transport glycerol, dihydroxyacetone, propanediol, urea and other small neutral molecules in physiologically important processes.  Some members of the family, including the yeast Fps1 protein (TC #1.A.8.5.1) and tobacco NtTIPa (TC #1.A.8.10.2) may transport both water and small solutes. A constriction within the pore, the aromatic/arginine (ar/R) selectivity filter, is thought to control solute permeability: narrow channels conduct water, whilst wider channels permit passage of solutes. Substrate discrimination depends on a complex interplay between the solute, pore size, and polarity (<span id='ref34550633.31889130' class='cite'>Kitchen <em>et al.</em> 2019</span>). Brain fluid can be secreted against an osmotic gradient, suggesting that conventional osmotic water flow via aquaporins may not fully describe transmembrane fluid transport in the brain (<span id='ref34553922.33846637' class='cite'>MacAulay 2021</span>). Aquaporins are essential to maintain motility and membrane lipid architecture during mammalian sperm capacitation (<span id='ref34553983.34540822' class='cite'>Delgado-Berm&uacute;dez <em>et al.</em> 2021</span>). The effects of Aloe-Emodin on the expression of brain aquaporins and secretion of neurotrophic factors has been reported (<span id='ref34556184.36982280' class='cite'>Liu <em>et al.</em> 2023</span>). AQPs are expressed in various parts of the body, and the unique roles they play in 
tumorigenesis, and the novel therapeutic approaches that could be 
adopted to treat carcinoma have been reviewed (<span id='ref34557782.38336645' class='cite'>Bhattacharjee <em>et al.</em> 2024</span>).</p>
<p><span id='ref34548692.30042691' class='cite'>Calamita <em>et al.</em> 2018</span> review the expression, regulation and physiological roles of AQPs in adipose tissue, liver and endocrine pancreas that are involved in energy metabolism. The review also summarizes the involvement of AQPs in metabolic disorders, such as obesity, diabetes and liver diseases. Challenges and recent advances related to pharmacological modulation of AQPs expression and function to control and treat metabolic diseases are discussed (<span id='ref34548692.30042691' class='cite'>Calamita <em>et al.</em> 2018</span>). <span id='ref34549109.30470864' class='cite'>Jain <em>et al.</em> 2018</span> have shown that an intra-helical salt-bridge in the Loop E half-helix can influence the transport properties of AQP1 and GlpF channels.&nbsp; AQPs are homotetramers with two conserved asparagine-proline-alanine (NPA) motifs embedding in the plasma membrane. The cellular functions of aquaporins are regulated mainly by posttranslational modifications, e.g., phosphorylation, ubiquitination, glycosylation, subcellular distribution, degradation, and protein interactions. Aquaporins, in particular, AQP2, play important roles in some disease conditions such as water loss and gain (<span id='ref34551113.32138947' class='cite'>Li <em>et al.</em> 2020</span>). The expression of AQP-1, -3, -4, -5, -8 and -9 were documented in the digestive system, where these six AQP isoforms serve essential roles including mediating the transmembrane water transport and regulating the secretion of gastrointestinal (GI) fluids, consequently facilitating the digestion and absorption of GI contents (<span id='ref34553262.33997172' class='cite'>Liao <em>et al.</em> 2021</span>). The expression levels of AQPs are controlled by various factors, and AQPs can stimulate various signaling pathways; however, aberrant expression of AQPs in the GI tracts are associated with the initiation and development of numerous diseases (<span id='ref34553262.33997172' class='cite'>Liao <em>et al.</em> 2021</span>). Altered iris aquaporin expression and aqueous humor osmolality in glaucoma have been compared (<span id='ref34553691.33616622' class='cite'>Huang <em>et al.</em> 2021</span>).</p>
<p>Zardoya and Villalba (<span id='ref1915.11443343' class='cite'>2001</span>) have conducted phylogenetic analyses of the MIP family, analyzing 153 homologues.  They divided the proteins into six major 'paralogous' groups: (1) GLPs, or glycerol-transporting channel proteins, which include mammalian AQP3, AQP7, and AQP9, several nematode paralogues, a yeast paralogue, and <em>Escherichia coli</em> GLP; (2) AQPs, or aquaporins, which include metazoan AQP0, AQP1, AQP2, AQP4, AQP5, and AQP6; (3) PIPs, or plasma membrane intrinsic proteins of plants, which include PIP1 and PIP2; (4) TIPs, or tonoplast intrinsic proteins of plants, which include &alpha;TIP, &gamma;TIP, and &delta;TIP; (5) NODs, or nodulins of plants; and (6) AQP8s, or metazoan aquaporin 8 proteins.  Of these groups, AQPs, PIPs, and TIPs cluster together as noted above. Wild and domesticated olive species have 52 and 79 genes encoding full-length AQP sequences, respectively (<span id='ref34553027.32545387' class='cite'>Faize <em>et al.</em> 2020</span>). They fall into five established subfamilies: PIP, TIP, NIP, SIP, and XIP and their substrate specificities and cellular localizations were predicted (<span id='ref34553027.32545387' class='cite'>Faize <em>et al.</em> 2020</span>). AQPs' selectivities are not exclusively shaped by pore-lining residues but are also determined by the relative arrangement of transmembrane helices (<span id='ref34554969.35802902' class='cite'>G&ouml;ssweiner-Mohr <em>et al.</em> 2022</span>). Aquaporins in astrocytes have been reviewed (<span id='ref34555139.36010640' class='cite'>Zhou <em>et al.</em> 2022</span>). The diverse range of substrates conducted by aquaporin family members, particularly those of human origin, have been reviewed (<span id='ref34555426.35986775' class='cite'>Sachdeva <em>et al.</em> 2022</span>), and current knowledge of the AQP interactomes and the molecular basis and functional significance of these protein-protein interactions in health and diseases have also been reviewed (<span id='ref34555447.36077012' class='cite'>T&ouml;rnroth-Horsefield <em>et al.</em> 2022</span>). Aquaporins in the digestive system have similarly been reviewed (<span id='ref34555894.36717492' class='cite'>Ye <em>et al.</em> 2023</span>). Their functions and mechanisms have also been reviewed (<span id='ref34555977.36672238' class='cite'>Calamita 2023</span>).</p>
<p>In agreement with their divergent sequences, human AQP1-9 have very different physiological functions.  They are involved in (1) nephrogenic diabetes insipidus, (2) brain water balance and hearing and (3) salivary secretion (<span id='ref2004.11406631' class='cite'>Li and Verkman, 2001</span>).&nbsp; Bacterial homologues also have diverse functions.&nbsp; Two proteins in<em> E. coli</em> function as water and glycerol transporters, respectively.&nbsp; <em>Lactobacillus plantarum</em> has 6 homologues, some of which transport water, glycerol and dihydroxyacetone, and some which transporter these compounds as well as D,L-lactic acid (<span id='ref10155.23799297' class='cite'>Bienert <em>et al.</em> 2013</span>).&nbsp; The pH sensitivities of Aqp0 channels in lenses of tetraploid and diploid teleosts have been reported (<span id='ref34543732.25667219' class='cite'>Chauvign&eacute; <em>et al.</em> 2015</span>).&nbsp; In the heart, AQPs are implicated in proper cardiac water homeostasis and energy balance as well as heart failure and arsenic cardiotoxicity (<span id='ref34549732.31027200' class='cite'>Verkerk <em>et al.</em> 2019</span>). Because of their glycerol permeability, aquaglyceroporins are involved in energy homeostasis. <span id='ref34553720.33689852' class='cite'>Calamita and Delporte 2021</span> provided an overview of the functional implication and control of aquaglyceroporins in tissues involved in energy metabolism, i.e. liver, adipose tissue and the endocrine pancreas. The expression, role and (dys)regulation of aquaglyceroporins in disorders affecting energy metabolism is also addressed. Aquaporins (AQPs) are involved in autoimmune diseases including neuromyelitis optica, Sjogren's syndrome and rheumatoid arthritis. Both autoantibodies against AQPs and altered expression and/or trafficking of AQPs in various tissue cell types as well as inflammatory cells play key roles in pathogenesis of autoimmune diseases. Detection of autoantibodies against AQP4 in the central nervous system has paved the way for a deeper understanding in disease pathophysiology (<span id='ref34555095.35690248' class='cite'>Delporte and Soyfoo 2022</span>).</p>
<p>Several reports of MIP family proteins transporting ions may or may not be physiologically significant.  For example, the influx of arsenite and antimonite via the Fps1 protein into yeast cells is well documented (<span id='ref1957.11442837' class='cite'>Wysocki <em>et al</em>., 2001</span>).  Similarly, these compounds are taken up via aquaporins in <em>Leishmania</em> (<span id='ref2928.15138256' class='cite'>Gourbal <em>et al</em>., 2004</span>).  Moreover, AQP6 of renal epithelia have been reported to transport anions at low pH (<span id='ref216.10647010' class='cite'>Yasui <em>et al</em>., 1999</span>).  Demonstration of the involvement of the cyanobacterial channel protein (TC #1.A.8.4.1) in copper homeostasis suggests that it may transport Cu<sup>2+</sup>.  Finally, Yang et al. (<span id='ref3224.16199569' class='cite'>2005</span>) showed that arsenite exists the <em>Mesorhizobium meliloti</em> cell by downhill movement through AqpS (1.A.8.15.1).  The physiological functions of many MIP family proteins are unknown. Differential expression of aquaporin genes and the influence of environmental hypertonicity on their expression in juveniles of air-breathing stinging catfish (Heteropneustes fossilis) has been examined (<span id='ref34555377.36096299' class='cite'>Chutia <em>et al.</em> 2022</span>). The results show that AQPs play crucial roles in maintaining the water and ionic balances under anisotonic conditions, even at the early developmental stages of stinging catfish.&nbsp; In humans, AQP1 is present in myoepithelial cells and in endothelial cells of 
small blood vessels; AQP3 shows basolateral plasmamembrane localization
 in glandular endpieces, and AQP5 is localized at the apical cytomembrane 
in serous and mucous glandular cells and at the lateral membrane in 
serous cells (<span id='ref34556058.36905859' class='cite'>Stoeckelhuber <em>et al.</em> 2023</span>).</p>
<p>MIP family channels consist of homotetramers (e.g., GlpF of <em>E. coli</em>; TC #1.A.8.1.1, AqpZ of <em>E. coli</em>; TC #1.A.8.3.1, and MIP or Aqp0 of <em>Bos taurus</em>; TC #1.A.8.8.1).  Each subunit spans the membrane six times as putative &alpha;-helices and arose from a 3-spanner-encoding genetic element by a tandem, intragenic duplication event.  The two halves of the proteins are therefore of opposite orientation in the membrane.  However, a well-conserved region between TMSs 2 and 3 and TMSs 5 and 6 dip into the membrane, each loop forming a half TMS. A voltage-related gating mechanism involving the conserved arginine of the channel's main constriction was captured for human aquaporins through molecular dynamics studies.&nbsp;<span id='ref34552158.32997337' class='cite'>Mom <em>et al.</em> 2020</span> showed that this voltage-gating is probably conserved among members of this family and that the underlying mechanism may explain part of plant AQPs diversity when contextualized to high ionic concentrations provoked by drought.</p>
<p>Several MIPs within all domains of life have been shown to facilitate the diffusion of reduced and non-charged species of the metalloids silicon, boron, arsenic and antimony (<span id='ref4856.18068370' class='cite'>Bienert <em>et al</em>., 2008</span>). Metalloids encompass a group of biologically important elements ranging from the essential to the highly toxic. Consequently, all organisms require efficient membrane transport systems to control the exchange of metalloids with the environment. Genetic evidence has demonstrated a crucial role for specific MIPs in metalloid homeostasis (<span id='ref4856.18068370' class='cite'>Bienert <em>et al</em>., 2008</span>). Permeation through each monomer of a tetrameric Aqp is consistent with closed and open states, introducing the term 'gating mechanism' into the field (<span id='ref34555506.36293170' class='cite'>Ozu <em>et al.</em> 2022</span>).</p>
<p>The crystal structure of the glycerol facilitator of <em>E. coli</em> was solved at 2.2 &Aring; resolution (<span id='ref218.11039922' class='cite'>Fu <em>et al</em>., 2000</span>).  Glycerol molecules line up in single file within the amphipathic channel.  In the narrow selectivity filter of the channel, the glycerol alkyl backbone is wedged against a hydrophobic corner, and successive hydroxyl groups form hydrogen bonds with a pair of acceptor and donor atoms.  The two conserved D-P-A motifs in the loops between TMSs 2 and 3 and TMSs 5 and 6 form the interface between the two duplicated halves of each subunit.  Thus each half of the protein forms 3.5 TMSs surrounding the channel.  The structure explains why GlpF is selectively permeable to straight chain carbohydrates, and why water and ions are excluded. Aquaporin-1 (AQP1) and the bacterial glycerol facilitator, GlpF can transport O<sub>2</sub>, CO<sub>2</sub>, NH<sub>3</sub>, glycerol, urea, and water to varying degrees. For small solutes permeating through AQP1, a remarkable anticorrelation between permeability and solute hydrophobicity was observed whereas the opposite trend was observed for permeation through the membrane (<span id='ref5549.18202181' class='cite'>Hub and Groot, 2008</span>). AQP1 is thus a selective filter for small polar solutes, whereas GlpF is highly permeable to small solutes and less permeable to larger solutes.&nbsp;</p>
<p>Aquaporin-1 (Aqp1) from the human red blood cell has been solved by x-ray crystallography to 3.8 &Aring; resolution (<span id='ref221.' class='cite'>Murata <em>et al</em>., 2000</span>).  The aqueous pathway is lined with conserved hydrophobic residues that permit rapid water transport.  Water selectivity is due to a constriction of the inner pore diameter to about 3 &Aring; over the span of a single residue, superficially similar to that in the glycerol facilitator of <em>E. coli.</em> The role of aquaporins in corneal healing post chemical injury has been described (<span id='ref34555920.36696947' class='cite'>Bhend <em>et al.</em> 2023</span>).</p>
<p>AqpZ, a homotetramer (tAqpZ) of four water-conducting channels that facilitate rapid water movements across the plasma membrane of <em>E. coli</em>, has been solved to 3.2 &Aring; resolution.  All channel-lining residues in the four monomeric channels are found orientated in nearly identical positions with one marked exception at the narrowest channel constriction, where the side chain of a conserved Arg-189 adopts two distinct conformational orientations.  In one of the four monomers, the guanidino group of Arg-189 points toward the periplasmic vestibule, opening up the constriction to accommodate the binding of a water molecule through a tridentate H-bond.  In the other three monomers, the Arg-189 guanidino group bends over to form an H-bond with carbonyl oxygen of Thr-183 occluding the channel.  Therefore, the tAqpZ structure reveals two distinct Arg-189 conformations associated with water permeation through the channel constrictions.  Alternating between the two Arg-189 conformations disrupts continuous flow of water, thus regulating the open probability of the water pore.  Further, the difference in Arg-189 displacements is correlated with a strong electron density found between the first transmembrane helices of two open channels, suggesting that the observed Arg-189 conformations are stabilized by asymmetrical subunit interactions in tAqpZ (<span id='ref3275.16239219' class='cite'>Jiang <em>et al</em>., 2006</span>).&nbsp;</p>
<p>Plants exhibits high diversity in aquaporin isoforms and broadly classified into five different subfamilies on the basis of phylogenetic distribution and subcellular occurrence: plasma membrane intrinsic proteins (PIPs), tonoplast intrinsic proteins (TIPs), nodulin 26-like proteins (NIPs), small basic intrinsic proteins (SIPs) and uncharacterized intrinsic proteins (XIPs) (<span id='ref34551058.32078896' class='cite'>Singh <em>et al.</em> 2020</span>). The gating mechanism of aquaporin channels is regulated by post-translational modifications such as phosphorylation, methylation, acetylation, glycosylation, and deamination. Aquaporin expression and transport functions are also modulated by the various phytohormone-mediated signalling in plants. Combined physiology and transcriptome analyses revealed the role of aquaporins in regulating hydraulic conductance in roots and leaves. Aquaporin activities during solute transport, plant development, abiotic stress response, and plant-microbe symbiosis have been reviewed (<span id='ref34551058.32078896' class='cite'>Singh <em>et al.</em> 2020</span>). Plant aquaporin gating is reversed by phosphorylation on intracellular loop D as estimated using evidence from molecular dynamics simulations (<span id='ref34557430.37762101' class='cite'>Mom <em>et al.</em> 2023</span>).</p>
<pre><em>	</em></pre>
<p>The 3-D structures of the open and closed forms of plant aquaporins, PIP1 and PIP2, have been solved (<span id='ref3402.16340961' class='cite'>T&ouml;rnroth-Horsefield <em>et al</em>., 2006</span>).  In the closed conformation, loop D caps the channel from the cytoplasm and thereby occludes the pore.  In the open conformation, loop D is displaced up to 16 &Aring;, and this movement opens a hydrophobic gate blocking the channel entrance from the cytoplasm.  These results reveal a molecular gating mechanism which appears conserved throughout all plant plasma membrane aquaporins.  In plants it regulates water intake/export in response to water availability and cytoplasmic pH during anoxia (<span id='ref3402.16340961' class='cite'>T&ouml;rnroth-Horsefield <em>et al</em>., 2006</span>).</p>
<p>43 AQP genes were identified in the forage crop <em>Medicago sativa</em>. The MsAQP proteins clustered into four subfamilies based on sequence similarity and phylogenetic relationship, including 17 TIPs, 14 NIPs, 9 PIPs and 3 SIPs (<span id='ref34554631.35328763' class='cite'>Luo <em>et al.</em> 2022</span>). Analyses on cis-acting elements in the promoter regions of MsAQP genes revealed the presence of multiple and diverse stress-responsive and hormone-responsive cis-acting elements. By analyzing the gene expression data of <em>M. truncatula</em>, ten representative MtAQP genes were responsive to NaCl or drought stress. By analyzing the sequence similarity and phylogenetic relationship, the corresponding ten salt- or drought-responsive AQP genes in <em>M. sativa</em>, including three MsTIPs, three MsPIPs and four MsNIPs. The qPCRs showed that the relative expression levels of these ten MsAQP genes responded differently to NaCl or drought treatment in <em>M. sativa</em>. Most MsAQP genes were preferentially expressed in roots or leaves (<span id='ref34554631.35328763' class='cite'>Luo <em>et al.</em> 2022</span>).</p>
<p>The MIP superfamily includes three functional subfamilies: aquaporins, aquaglyceroporins and S-aquaporins. (1) The aquaporins (AQPs) are water selective. (2) The aquaglyceroporins are permeable to water, but also to other small uncharged molecules. (3) The third subfamily, with little conserved amino acid sequences around the NPA boxes, include 'superaquaporins' (S-aquaporins).The phylogeny of insect MIP family channels has been published (<span id='ref34544263.26183829' class='cite'>Finn <em>et al.</em> 2015</span>). The arylsulfonamide AqB011 which selectively blocks the central ion pore 
of mammalian AQP1 has been shown to impair migration of HT29 colon 
cancer cells. Traditional herbal medicines are sources of selective AQP1
 inhibitors that also slow cancer cell migration (<span id='ref34547639.29193257' class='cite'>Kourghi <em>et al.</em> 2018</span>).</p>
<p>13 isoforms of mammalian aquaporins (AQP0 - AQP12),are known, nine of which localize to different parts of the renal tubular epithelium.&nbsp; Additional transport functions of renal AQPs (AQP3, AQP6, AQP7 and AQP8) are known. Aquaglyceroporins are most probably key elements in the renal regulation of nitrogen balance and maintenance of the correct pH of body fluids (<span id='ref34546659.27226178' class='cite'>Michalek 2016</span>). In human sperm, AQP3 and AQP11 are expressed mainly in the tail, AQP7 in the head and AQP8 in the midpiece (<span id='ref34553488.34087390' class='cite'>Pellavio and Laforenza 2021</span>). AQPs are important for the normal functioning of sperm to ensure normal fertility. AQP3, AQP7 and AQP11 are involved in sperm volume regulation, a key role for fertility because osmoadaptation protects the sperm against swelling and tail bending that could affect sperm motility. AQP8 has a fundamental role in regulating the elimination of hydrogen peroxide, the most abundant reactive oxygen species (ROS), and therefore plays a role in the response to oxidative stress (<span id='ref34553488.34087390' class='cite'>Pellavio and Laforenza 2021</span>).&nbsp;</p>
<p>Otitis media (OM) refers to inflammatory diseases of the middle ear (ME), regardless of cause or pathological mechanism. The expression of aquaporins (AQPs) in the ME and Eustachian tube (ET) is relevant. Eleven types of AQPs, AQP1 to AQP11, have been found to be expressed in mammalian ME and ET (<span id='ref34547476.29039751' class='cite'>Jung <em>et al.</em> 2017</span>). The distribution and levels of expression of AQPs depend on the presence or absence of inflammation. Fluid accumulation in the ME and ET is a common mechanism for all types of OM, causing edema in the tissue and inducing inflammation involving various AQPs. The expression patterns of several AQPs, especially AQP1, 4 and 5, may have immunological functions in OM.</p>
<p>Some classes of AQPs conduct ions, glycerol, urea, CO<sub>2</sub> , nitric oxide, and other small solutes. Ion channel activity has been demonstrated for mammalian AQPs 0, 1, 6, <em>Drosophila</em> big brain (BIB), soybean nodulin 26, and rockcress AtPIP2;1 (<span id='ref34547639.29193257' class='cite'>Kourghi <em>et al.</em> 2017</span>). Classification of AQPs into three categories 
(orthodox AQPs, aquaglyceroporins and superaquaporins) is based on their
 sequence similarities and substrate selectivities. In the male reproductive
 tract of mammals, most AQPs (except AQP6 and <span class='highlight'>AQP12</span>)
 are found in different organs (including testis, efferent ducts and 
epididymis). AQP1 and AQP9 are the most abundant AQPs in the efferent 
ducts and epididymis and play a crucial role for the 
secretion/reabsorption dynamics of luminal fluid during sperm transport 
and maturation. AQP3, AQP7, AQP8 and AQP11 are the most abundant AQPs in
 sperm and are involved in the regulation of their volumes, which is 
required for the differentiation of spermatids into spermatozoa during 
spermatogenesis, as well as in sperm transit along environments of 
different osmolality (male and female reproductive tracts). Mounting evidence indicates that AQP3, AQP7 and AQP11 are 
involved in cryotolerance as well as the sperm response to variations of osmolality and to 
freeze-thawing procedures (<span id='ref34548120.29052330' class='cite'>Yeste <em>et al.</em> 2017</span>).</p>
<p>In mammals, aquaporins are subdivided into classical aquaporins that are permeable to water; aquaglyceroporins that are permeable to water, glycerol and urea; peroxiporins that facilitate the diffusion of H<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub> through cell membranes; and so called unorthodox aquaporins. Aquaporins ensure important physiological functions in both exocrine and endocrine pancreas and are involved in pancreatic fluid and insulin secretion. Modification of aquaporin expression and/or subcellular localization may be involved in the pathogenesis of pancreatic insufficiencies, diabetes and pancreatic cancer (<span id='ref34551042.31614661' class='cite'>Arsenijevic <em>et al.</em> 2019</span>).</p>
<p>Mechanisms that drive the development of multiple inflammatory diseases that occur in the nose and contribute to the process of olfactory recognition of compounds entering the nasal cavity involve the action of water channels such as AQPs.&nbsp;<span id='ref34552062.32824013' class='cite'>Jung <em>et al.</em> 2020</span> reviewed the relationship between AQPs and rhinologic conditions, focusing on the current state of knowledge and mechanisms that link AQPs and rhinologic conditions. Key conclusions include the following: (1) Various AQPs are expressed in both nasal mucosa and olfactory mucosa; (2) the expression of AQPs in these tissues is different in inflammatory diseases such as AR or CRS, as compared with that in normal tissues; (3) the expression of AQPs in CRS differs depending on the presence or absence of nasal polyps; and (4) the expression of AQPs in tissues associated with olfaction is different from that in the respiratory epithelium.</p>
<p>Water homeostasis plays a crucial role in different reproductive processes, e.g., oocyte transport, hormonal secretion, completion of successful fertilization, blastocyst formation, pregnancy, and birth (<span id='ref34552445.33271827' class='cite'>Kordowitzki <em>et al.</em> 2020</span>). Further, aquaporins are involved in the process of spermatogenesis, and they have been reported to be involved in the storage of spermatozoa. Aquaporins are relevant for seveeral physiological functions in the female reproductive system, and they are relevant to different pathologies in the female reproductive system. Four <em>Impatiens walleriana</em> aquaporins: IwPIP1;4, IwPIP2;2, IwPIP2;7 and IwTIP4;1, have been characteerized (<span id='ref34552600.33466920' class='cite'>Đurić <em>et al.</em> 2021</span>). Drought stress&nbsp; affected the aquaporin expression in <em>I. walleriana</em> leaves, which was up- or downregulated depending on stress intensity. Expression of IwPIP2;7 was the most affected of these four aquaporins. At 15% and 5% soil moisture and recovery from 15% and 5% soil moisture, IwPIP2;7 expression significantly decreased and increased, respectively. Aquaporins IwPIP1;4 and IwTIP4;1 had lower expression than IwPIP2;7, with moderate expression changes in response to drought and recovery, while IwPIP2;2 expression was of significance only in recovered plants (<span id='ref34552600.33466920' class='cite'>Đurić <em>et al.</em> 2021</span>).</p>
<p>Humans contain 13 AQPs (AQP0-AQP12) which are divided into three sub-classes namely orthodox aquaporin (AQP0, 1, 2, 4, 5, 6, and 8), aquaglyceroporin (AQP3, 7, 9, and 10) and super or unorthodox aquaporin (AQP11 and 12) based on their pore selectivity. They are involved in a wide variety of non-infectious diseases including cancer, renal dysfunction, neurological disorders, epilepsy, skin diseases, metabolic syndrome, and even cardiac diseases. AQPs can be regulated by microbial and parasitic infections that suggest their involvement in microbial pathogenesis, inflammation-associated responses and AQP-mediated cell water homeostasis. In a review,&nbsp;<span id='ref34553041.33796134' class='cite'>Azad <em>et al.</em> 2021</span> examine the involvement of AQPs in infectious and non-infectious diseases and potential AQPs-target modulators. AQP structures, tissue-specific distributions and physiological relevance, functional diversity and regulation were considered. Human AQPs play roles in  edema, glaucoma, nephrogenic diabetes insipidus, oxidative stress, sepsis, cancer, and metabolic dysfunctions (<span id='ref34555120.35883453' class='cite'>da Silva <em>et al.</em> 2022</span>). The 13 AQPs draw cell lineage-specific expression patterns related to cell native functions. Compelling evidence indicates that AQPs have potential as biomarkers and targets for therapeutic intervention. AQP9 is most expressed in the liver, influencing general metabolic and redox balance due to its aquaglyceroporin and peroxiporin activities. AQP9 levels in other tissues are altered in several human diseases, such as liver injury, inflammation, cancer, infertility, and immune disorders (<span id='ref34555120.35883453' class='cite'>da Silva <em>et al.</em> 2022</span>). Aqp activities are sensitive to mercury ions (Hg<sup>2+</sup>). While most aquaporins are inhibited by Hg<sup>2+</sup>, several are activated.&nbsp;<span id='ref34555649.36413513' class='cite'>Xie <em>et al.</em> 2022</span> investigated AqpZ of <em>E. coli</em> (TC# 1.A.8.3.1) inhibition and human AQP6 (TC# 1.A.8.8.4) activation. Based on the structure of the Hg-AqpZ complex, they found that pore closure was caused by mercury-induced conformational changes of the key residue R189 in the selectivity filter region, while pore opening was caused by conformational changes of residues H181 and R196 in the selectivity filter region in AQP6. Both conformational changes were caused by the disruption of the H-bond network of R189/R196 by mercury (<span id='ref34555649.36413513' class='cite'>Xie <em>et al.</em> 2022</span>).&nbsp;</p>
<p>As small ectotherms, insects need to cope with the challenges of winter cold by regulating their water content. Aquaporins (AQPs) are key players to enhance cold resistance by mediating essential homeostatic processes in many animals but remain poorly characterized in insects. <em>Agriphila aeneociliella</em> is a winter wheat pest in China, and its early-stage larvae have strong tolerance to low temperature stress. Six AQP genes were identified, which belong to five AQP subfamilies (RPIP, Eglp, AQP12L, PRIP, DRIP) (<span id='ref34557601.38025732' class='cite'>Zhao <em>et al.</em> 2023</span>). All of them contained six hydrophobic TMSs and two relatively conservative Asparagine-Proline-Alanine motifs. The three-dimensional homology modeling showed that the six TMSs folded into an hourglass-like shape, and the imperceptible replacement of four ar/R residues in the contraction region had critical effects on changing the pore size of the channels. Moreover, the transcript levels of AaAQPs 1, 3, and 6 increased significantly with the treatment time below 0  degrees C. Combined with the results of pore radius variations, it was suggested that AaAQP1 and AaAQP3 are the key anti-hypothermia proteins in <em>A. aeneociliella </em>by regulating rapid cell dehydration and allowing the influx of extracellular cold resistance molecules, thus avoiding death in winter.</p>
<p>The generalized transport reaction for channel proteins of the MIP family is:</p>

Plants (Basel) 10:.<BR><HR></TD></TR><TR><TD COLSPAN=5><B>Examples:</B><P></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD>TC#</TD><TD>Name</TD><TD>Organismal Type</TD><TD>Example</TD></TR><TR><TD COLSPAN=5><B>Examples:</B><P></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD>TC#</TD><TD>Name</TD><TD>Organismal Type</TD><TD>Example</TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.8.1.1"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.8.1.1&tcname=">1.A.8.1.1</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Glycerol facilitator, GlpF. Transports various polyols with decreasing rates as size increases (<span id="ref34545553.6998951" class="cite">Heller <em>et al.</em> 1980</span>); also transports arsenite (As(III) and antimonite (Sb(III)) (<span id="ref2879.14970228" class="cite">Meng <em>et al</em>., 2004</span>). Oligomerization may play a role in determining the rates of transport (<span id="ref34550715.30791644" class="cite">Klein <em>et al.</em> 2019</span>). AQP water permeability through GlpF can be regulated by lipid bilayer asymmetry and the transmembrane potential. The conserved Arg in the selectivity filter and positions and dynamics of multiple other pore lining residues modulate water passage through GlpF (<span id="ref34555493.36173178" class="cite">Pluhackova <em>et al.</em> 2022</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Gram-negative bacteria</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>GlpF of <em>E. coli</em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.8.1.2"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.8.1.2&tcname=">1.A.8.1.2</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Aqp1 of 270 aas and 6 TMSs. Induced by NH<sub>3</sub> but not CO<sub>2</sub>, but transports both gases. Aqp1 is found in the plasma membrae as well as the ER/chloroplast. Aqp1 may be involved in photoprotection. It may facilitate the efflux of NH3, preventing the uncoupling effect of high intracellular ammonia concentrations (<span id="ref34548614.30076224" class="cite">Matsui <em>et al.</em> 2018</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Aqp1 of <em>Phaeodactylum tricornutum</em>, a marine photoautotrophic diatoms</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD COLSPAN=5><B>Examples:</B><P></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD>TC#</TD><TD>Name</TD><TD>Organismal Type</TD><TD>Example</TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.8.10.1"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.8.10.1&tcname=">1.A.8.10.1</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Tonoplast intrinsic protein, TIPA or Tip3.1, of 268 aas and 6 TMSs.&nbsp; Phylogenetic distribution, structure, transport dynamics, gating mechanism, sub-cellular localization, tissue-specific expression, and co-expression of TIPs have been reviewed to define their versatile role in plants (<span id="ref34552855.33723851" class="cite">Sudhakaran <em>et al.</em> 2021</span>). Based on the phylogenetic distribution, TIPs are classified into five distinct groups with aromatic-arginine (Ar/R) selectivity filters, typical pore-morphology, and tissue-specific gene expression patterns. The tissue-specific expression of TIPs is conserved among diverse plant species, especially for TIP3s, which are expressed exclusively in seeds. The solute specificities of TIPs plays a role in physiological processes like stomatal movement and vacuolar sequestration as well as in alleviating environmental stress. TIPs also play a role in growth and developmental processes like radicle protrusion, anther dehiscence, seed germination, cell elongation, and expansion. The gating mechanism of TIPs regulates the solute flow in response to external signals, which helps to maintain the physiological functions of the cell (<span id="ref34552855.33723851" class="cite">Sudhakaran <em>et al.</em> 2021</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Plants</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>TIP of <em>Arabidopsis thaliana</em> (P26587)</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.8.10.10"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.8.10.10&tcname=">1.A.8.10.10</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Aquaporin TIP2-1 (Delta-tonoplast intrinsic protein) (Delta-TIP) (Tonoplast intrinsic protein 2-1) (AtTIP2;1) (<span id="ref34549362.8624437" class="cite">Daniels <em>et al.</em> 1996</span>). Transports water and ammonia, and can be activated by mercury (<span id="ref34549360.27625657" class="cite">Kirscht <em>et al.</em> 2016</span>). The 3-d structure is known to 1.2&Aring; resolution (<span id="ref34549361.27028365" class="cite">Kirscht <em>et al.</em> 2016</span>). <span>It may participate in vacuolar compartmentation and detoxification of ammonium.</span></p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Plants</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>TIP2-1 of <em>Arabidopsis thaliana </em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" H<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, and it is present in the inner membrane of mitochondria and the plasma membrane (<span id="ref3593.17105724" class="cite">Bienert <em>et al</em>., 2007</span>; <span id="ref3594.17189259" class="cite">Saparov <em>et al</em>., 2007</span>; <span id="ref6862.20132793" class="cite">Soria <em>et al.</em>, 2010</span>). It transports metaloids such as arsenous acid (arsenic) and silisic acid (silicon).&nbsp; The 3-D structure has been determined (<span id="ref34556415.37164213" class="cite">Sharma <em>et al.</em> 2023</span>). This protein is most similar to TC# 1.A.8.12.2 within TCDB.</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>NIP2.1 of <em>Zea mays</em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.8.12.2"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.8.12.2&tcname=">1.A.8.12.2</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>The silicon (silicic acid) (undissociated form) transporter, Lsi1 (<a class="reflink" href="result.php?tc=1.A.8#ref4494">Ma <em>et al</em>., 2007a</a>, b; <a class="reflink" href="result.php?tc=1.A.8#ref4898">Mitani <em>et al</em>., 2008</a>). The barley orthologue Lsi1 (also called NIP2-1) is also a silicon (silicic acid) uptake channel (<a class="reflink" href="result.php?tc=1.A.8#ref6645">Chiba <em>et al.</em>, 2009</a>). Rice Lsi1 also transports arsenite and pentavalent mono and dimethyl arsenite (<a class="reflink" href="result.php?tc=1.A.8#ref6763">Li <em>et al.</em>, 2009</a>). In addition to silicon (Si), selenite (Se) uptake is mediated by Lsi1 (<a class="reflink" href="result.php?tc=1.A.8#ref7211">Zhao <em>et al.</em>, 2010</a>).&nbsp; Physicochemical and transcriptomic responses of <em>Lactobacillus brevis</em> JLD715 to sodium selenite have been reported (<span id="ref34552688.33417239" class="cite">Yang <em>et al.</em> 2021</span>). Many of the world's most important food crops such as rice, barley and maize accumulate silicon (Si) to high levels, resulting in better plant growth and crop yields (<span id="ref34553797.34487790" class="cite">van den Berg <em>et al.</em> 2021</span>). The first step in Si accumulation is the uptake of silicic acid by the roots, a process mediated by the NIP subfamily of aquaporins, also named metalloid porins.&nbsp;<span id="ref34553797.34487790" class="cite">van den Berg <em>et al.</em> 2021</span> presented the X-ray crystal structure of the archetypal NIP family member from <em>Oryza sativa</em> (OsNIP2;1). The OsNIP2;1 channel is closed in the crystal structure by the cytoplasmic loop D, which is known to regulate channel opening in classical plant aquaporins. The structure reveals a novel, five-residue extracellular selectivity filter with a large diameter. Unbiased molecular dynamics simulations show a rapid opening of the channel to visualise how silicic acid interacts with the selectivity filter prior to transmembrane diffusion. These results may enable detailed structure-function studies of metalloid porins, including the basis of their substrate selectivity (<span id="ref34553797.34487790" class="cite">van den Berg <em>et al.</em> 2021</span>). Silicon (Si), the most abundant mineral element in the earth's crust, is taken up by plant roots in the form of silicic acid through Low silicon rice 1 (Lsi1). Lsi1 belongs to the Nodulin 26-like intrinsic protein subfamily and shows high selectivity for silicic acid. The crystal structure of rice Lsi1 at a resolution of 1.8 &Aring; reveals transmembrane helical orientations different from other aquaporins, characterized by a unique, widely opened, and hydrophilic selectivity filter composed of five residues. Structural, functional, and theoretical investigations provided a solid basis for the Si uptake mechanism in plants (<span id="ref34554030.34716344" class="cite">Saitoh <em>et al.</em> 2021</span>).</p>

Lactic acid transport is induced by anoxic stress (<span id="ref4216.17584741" class="cite">Choi and Roberts, 2007</span>).</TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC>Plants </TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC>NIP2;1 of <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i> (Q8W037)</TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.8.12.5"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.8.12.5&tcname=">1.A.8.12.5</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>The silicon (silicic acid) transporter, Nip2-2 (Nip2;2) (<span id="ref4898.18214526" class="cite">Mitani <em>et al</em>., 2008</span>). The grape ortholog appears to transport the same molecules including water and glycerol, but also arsenate, borate, selenate and cadmium (<span id="ref34550962.31963923" class="cite">Sabir <em>et al.</em> 2020</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Plants</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>NIP1-1 of <em>Arabidopsis thaliana </em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.8.12.9"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.8.12.9&tcname=">1.A.8.12.9</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Aquaporin NIP6-1 (NOD26-like intrinsic protein 6-1) (AtNIP6;1).&nbsp; <span id="ref8458.22369250" class="cite">Araya-Secchi <em>et al.</em>, 2011</span>). Its 3-d structure has been determined to 1.7 &Aring;. In AqpM, isoleucine replaces a key histidine residue found in the lumen of water channels, which becomes a glycine residue in aquaglyceroporins. As a result of this and other side-chain substituents in the walls of the channel, the channel is intermediate in size and exhibits differentially tuned electrostatics when compared with the other subfamilies (<span id="ref34548812.16361443" class="cite">Lee <em>et al.</em> 2005</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Archaea</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>AqpM of <em>Methanothermobacter marburgensis</em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.8.13.3"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.8.13.3&tcname=">1.A.8.13.3</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Putative aquaporin, GlpF5, of  216 aas; probably transports water, glycerol and dihydroxyacetone (<a class="reflink" href="result.php?tc=1.A.8#ref10155">Bienert <em>et al.</em> 2013</a>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Firmicutes</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>GlpF5 of <em>Lactobacillus plantarum</em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.8.13.4"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.8.13.4&tcname=">1.A.8.13.4</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Aquaporin, Aqp, of 222 aas and 6 TMSs.&nbsp; It functions in hydrogen peroxide (H<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>) export from the cell, relieving oxidative stress (<span id="ref34550650.30705089" class="cite">Tong <em>et al.</em> 2019</span>). It is an H<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>-inducible bacterial "peroxiporin".</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Aqp of <em>Streptococcus oligofermentans </em>or <em>Streptococcus cristatus</em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.8.13.5"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.8.13.5&tcname=">1.A.8.13.5</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Aquaporin, Aqp9, of 231 aas and 6 TMSs.&nbsp; This protein  co-localizes with
 the vacuolar proton pyrophosphatase to acidocalcisomes and the 
contractile vacuole complex (<a class="reflink" href="http://www.tcdb.org/search/result.php?tc=1.A.8#ref34551498">Montalvetti <em>et al.</em> 2004</a>) which are involved in osmoregulation (<a class="reflink" href="http://www.tcdb.org/search/result.php?tc=1.A.8#ref34551499">Rohloff <em>et al.</em> 2004</a>). Acidocalcisomes function as storage sites for cations and phosphorus, 
participate in PPi and poly P metabolism as well as volume regulation and are
 essential for virulence. A signalling pathway involving cyclic AMP 
generation is important for fusion of acidocalcisomes to the contractile
 vacuole complex, transference of aquaporin and volume regulation (<a class="reflink" href="http://www.tcdb.org/search/result.php?tc=1.A.8#ref34551500">Docampo <em>et al.</em> 2011</a>).
 Hyperosmotic stress induces aquaporin-dependent cell shrinkage, 
polyphosphate synthesis, amino acid accumulation, and global gene 
expression changes in <em>Trypanosoma cruzi </em>(<a class="reflink" href="http://www.tcdb.org/search/result.php?tc=1.A.8#ref34551501">Li <em>et al.</em> 2011</a>). <em>Plasmodium</em> spp. express a single AQP, <em>Toxoplasma gondii</em> two, while 
<em>Trypanosoma cruzi</em> and <em>Leishamnia </em>spp. encode up to five AQPs. Their AQPs
 are thought to import metabolic precursors and simultaneously to 
dispose of waste and to help parasites survive osmotic stress (<a class="reflink" href="http://www.tcdb.org/search/result.php?tc=1.A.8#ref34551502">Von B&uuml;low and Beitz 2015</a>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Aqp9 of <em>Trypanosoma cruzi</em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.8.13.6"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.8.13.6&tcname=">1.A.8.13.6</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Aquaporin of 214 aas and 6 TMSs in a 2 + 1 + 2 + 1 TMS arrangement.</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Aqp of Bacteroidia bacterium (subsurface metagenome)</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD COLSPAN=5><B>Examples:</B><P></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD>TC#</TD><TD>Name</TD><TD>Organismal Type</TD><TD>Example</TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.8.14.1"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.8.14.1&tcname=">1.A.8.14.1</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Putative aquaporin, Aqp2, with a large 300 residue amino terminal hydrophilic domain. It is important for maintaining osmotic balance during mating-induced yeast cell fusion and for tolerating hypoosmotic shock; it also transports arsenite and antimonite). FPS1 is a homotetramer (<span id="ref8137.22030956" class="cite">Beese-Sims <em>et al.</em>, 2011</span>). It is important for osmo-adaptation by regulating intracellular glycerol levels during changes in external osmolarity. Upon high osmolarity conditions, yeast accumulate glycerol by increased production of the osmolyte and by restricting glycerol efflux through Fps1. The extended cytosolic termini of Fps1 contain short domains that are important for regulating glycerol flux through the channel (<span id="ref34554295.14752103" class="cite">Hedfalk <em>et al.</em> 2004</span>). The transmembrane core of the protein plays an equally important role (<span id="ref8726.22593571" class="cite">Geijer <em>et al.</em>, 2012</span>).&nbsp; The MAP kinase, Slt2, physically interacts
with Fps1, and this interaction, dependent on phosphorylation of S537, regulates arsenite uptake (<span id="ref34545807.27607883" class="cite">Ahmadpour <em>et al.</em> 2016</span>). The N-terminal regulatory domain and the B-loop may interact in channel control (<span id="ref34554583.14764093" class="cite">Karlgren <em>et al.</em> 2004</span>). Fps1 resides in multi tetrameric clusters, and hyperosmotic and oxidative stress conditions cause Fps1 reorganization, and rapid exposure to hydrogen peroxide causes Fps1 degradation (<span id="ref34554798.32387484" class="cite">Shashkova <em>et al.</em> 2021</span>). Activation of the CWI pathway through high hydrostatic pressure, enhances glycerol efflux via Fps1 in <em>Saccharomyces cerevisiae</em> (see family 9.B.454 in TCDB).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><hr />

It may be the only aquaporin capable of H<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub> transport and signalling.<sub><br /></sub></p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>AqpF of <em>Aspergillus niger</em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD COLSPAN=5><B>Examples:</B><P></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD>TC#</TD><TD>Name</TD><TD>Organismal Type</TD><TD>Example</TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.8.8.1"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.8.8.1&tcname=">1.A.8.8.1</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Aquaporin 1 (CO<sub>2</sub>-, O<sub>2</sub>-, H<sub>2</sub>0<sub>2-</sub> and nitrous oxide-permeable and water-selective) (<span>Zwiazek <em>et al.</em> 2017</span>; <span id="ref34552030.32063362" class="cite">Varadaraj and Kumari 2020</span>). Aquaporin-1 tunes pain perception by interacting with Na(v)1.8 Na<sup>+</sup> channels in dorsal root ganglion neurons (<a>Zhang and Verkman, 2010</a>). It is upregulated in skeletal muscle in muscular dystrophy (<span>Au <em>et al.</em> 2008</span>). AQP1 has been reported to
first insert as a four-helical intermediate, where helices 2 and 4 are not inserted into the membrane.
In a second step this intermediate is folded into a six-helical topology. During this process, the
orientation of the third helix is inverted, and it can shift out the membrane core (<a>Virkki <em>et al.</em> 2014</a>).&nbsp; Its synthesis is regluated by Kruppel-like factor 2 (KLF2; Q9Y5W3) which also interacts directly with Aqp1 (<span>Fontijn <em>et al.</em> 2015</span>). A nanoscale ion pump has been derived artificially from Aqp1 (<span>Decker <em>et al.</em> 2017</span>). Mammalian AQP1 channels, activated by cyclic GMP, can carry non-selective monovalent cation currents, selectively blocked by arylsulfonamide compounds AqB007 (IC50 170 muM) and AqB011 (IC50 14 muM). Loop D-domain amino acids activate the channel for ion coductance (<span>Kourghi <em>et al.</em> 2018</span>). Water flux through AQP1s is inhibited by 1 - 10 m&mu;M acetozolaminde (<span>Gao <em>et al.</em> 2006</span>). Aqp1 transports reactive oxygen and nitrogen species (RONS) which may induce oxidative stress in the cell interior. These RONS include both hydrophilic (H<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub> and OH) and hydrophobic (NO<sub>2</sub> and NO) RONS (<span id="ref34549984.31316715" class="cite">Yusupov <em>et al.</em> 2019</span>).&nbsp; The position of the Arg-195 side chain shows a number of interactions for loop C (<span id="ref34550076.31411472" class="cite">Dingwell <em>et al.</em> 2019</span>). AQP1 play vital roles in cellular homeostasis at rest and during endurance running exercises (<span id="ref34550228.31486928" class="cite">Rivera and Fahey 2019</span>). AQP1 and AQP4 activities correlate with the severity of hydrocephalus induced by subarachnoid haemorrhage (<span id="ref34551078.31556577" class="cite">Long <em>et al.</em> 2019</span>). AQPs are related to osmoregulation and play a critical role in maturation, cryo-stability and motility activation in boar spermatozoa (<span id="ref34551362.31835821" class="cite">Delgado-Berm&uacute;dez <em>et al.</em> 2019</span>). In foetal kidney, AQP1 expression appeared in the apical and basolateral parts of cells, lining the proximal convoluted tubules and the descending limb of Henle's loop, then in the tubule pole of Bowman's capsule (<span id="ref34551437.31658316" class="cite">R&aacute;duly <em>et al.</em> 2019</span>). Inhibition of aquaporin-1 prevents myocardial remodeling by blocking the transmembrane transport of hydrogen peroxide (<span id="ref34552192.33028705" class="cite">Montiel <em>et al.</em> 2020</span>). AQP1 Is up-regulated by hypoxia and leads to increased cell water permeability, motility, and migration in neuroblastoma (<span id="ref34552826.33644043" class="cite">Huo <em>et al.</em> 2021</span>). Aqp1 allows the transport of CO<sub>2</sub> across membranes (<span id="ref34552886.33633837" class="cite">Michenkova <em>et al.</em> 2021</span>). Down-regulation of aquaporin-1 mediates a microglial phenotype switch affecting glioma growth (<span id="ref34553037.33058832" class="cite">Hu <em>et al.</em> 2020</span>). AQP1 expression is down-regulated following repeated exposure of UVB via MEK/ERK activation pathways, and this AQP1 reduction leads to changes of physiological functions in dermal fibroblasts (<span id="ref34553129.33911740" class="cite">Kim <em>et al.</em> 2020</span>). AQP1 and AQP7 are differentially regulated under hyperosmotic stress conditions, and AQP1 acts as an osmotic stress sensor and response factor (<span id="ref34553766.33506920" class="cite">Aggeli <em>et al.</em> 2021</span>). AQP1 plays a role in the pathogenesis of Wilms' tumor (<span id="ref34557566.37904849" class="cite">Liu <em>et al.</em> 2023</span>).&nbsp; Aquaporin-1 plays a role in cell proliferation, apoptosis, and pyroptosis of Wilms' tumor cells (<span id="ref34557781.38334883" class="cite">Liu <em>et al.</em> 2024</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Animals</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Aquaporin 1 (AQP1) of <em>Homo sapiens </em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.8.8.10"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.8.8.10&tcname=">1.A.8.8.10</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Water and urea transporting aquaporin (cockroach) (<span id="ref8367.21993792" class="cite">Herraiz <em>et al.</em>, 2011</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Animals</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Aquaporin of <em>Blatella germanica</em>&nbsp;(G8YY04)</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.8.8.11"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.8.8.11&tcname=">1.A.8.8.11</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Water channel, Aqp1; inhibited by HgCl<sub>2</sub>&nbsp;and tetraethylammonium. chemokines such as CCL24 and CCL22 through regulating the amount of cellular H<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub> in M2 polarized
alveolar macrophages, implying a role of AQP3 in asthma (<span id="ref34545420.27165276" class="cite">Ikezoe <em>et al.</em> 2016</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Aqp3 of <em>Opisthorchis viverrini </em>(liver fluke)</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.8.8.18"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.8.8.18&tcname=">1.A.8.8.18</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Aqp-x2 water channel in the luminal epithelium of urinary bladder cells and lungs.&nbsp; Responsive to Vasotocin (AVT) (<span id="ref34545538.25220852" class="cite">Shibata <em>et al.</em> 2015</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Aqp-x2 of <em>Xenopus laevis</em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.8.8.19"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.8.8.19&tcname=">1.A.8.8.19</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Contractile vacuole aquaporin of 295 aas and 6 TMSs, Aqp.&nbsp; Shown to transport water, accounting for the high water permeability of the contractile vacuole (<span id="ref34545851.18004980" class="cite">Nishihara <em>et al.</em> 2008</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Aqp of <em>Amoeba proteus </em>(Amoeba)<em> (Chaos diffluens)</em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.8.8.2"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.8.8.2&tcname=">1.A.8.8.2</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>The lens fiber MIP aquaporin (Aqp0) of <em>B. taurus</em> (forms membrane junctions <em>in vivo</em> and double layered crystals <em>in vitro</em> that resemble the <em>in vivo</em> junctions).  The water pore is closed in the <em>in vitro</em> structure (<span id="ref2801.15141214" class="cite">Gonen et al., 2004b</span>). Forms human cataract lens membranes (<span id="ref4434.17920625" class="cite">Buzhynskyy <em>et al</em>., 2007</span>; <span id="ref8593.21647270" class="cite">Yang <em>et al.</em>, 2011</span>).&nbsp; A mutation that causes congenital dominant lens cataracts has been identified (<span id="ref34545345.18501347" class="cite">Varadaraj <em>et al.</em> 2008</span>). AqpO catalyzes Zn<sup>2+</sup>-modulated water permeability as a cooperative tetramer (<span id="ref4447.17938229" class="cite">Nemeth-Cahalan <em>et al</em>., 2007</span>). It transports ascorbic acid (<span id="ref8136.22020074" class="cite">Nakazawa <em>et al.</em>, 2011</span>). The Detergent organization around solubilized aquaporin-0 using Small Angle X-ray Scattering has been reported (<span id="ref8717.22621369" class="cite">Berthaud <em>et al.</em>, 2012</span>).&nbsp; Aquaporin 0 (AQP0) in the eye lens is truncated by proteolytic cleavage during lens maturation. This truncated AQP0 is no longer a water channel (<span id="ref34544858.26662491" class="cite">Berthaud <em>et al.</em> 2015</span>).&nbsp; A mutation that causes congenital dominant lens cataracts has been identified (<span id="ref34545345.18501347" class="cite">Varadaraj <em>et al.</em> 2008</span>). Cataractogenesis in MIP mutants are probably caused by defects in MIP gene expression in mice (<span id="ref34547430.28442635" class="cite">Takahashi <em>et al.</em> 2017</span>). This may be caused by the ability of Aqp0 (as well as Aqp1 and Aqp5) to transport hydrogen peroxide (H<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>) which can cause cataracts (<span id="ref34552030.32063362" class="cite">Varadaraj and Kumari 2020</span>).&nbsp; An automated data processing and analysis pipeline for transmembrane proteins including Aqp0 in detergent solutions has been presented (<span id="ref34551515.32415234" class="cite">Molodenskiy <em>et al.</em> 2020</span>). EphA2 is required for normal Cx50 localization to the cell membrane, and
 conductance of lens fiber cells requires normal Eph-ephrin signaling 
and water channel (Aqp0) localization (<span id="ref34554181.34899394" class="cite">Cheng <em>et al.</em> 2021</span>). The local curvature of cellular membranes acts as a driving force for 
the targeting of membrane-associated proteins to specific membrane 
domains, as well as a sorting mechanism for transmembrane proteins, as 
demonstrated for Aqp0; AQP0 causes small negative curvature (<span id="ref34554505.35122737" class="cite">Kluge <em>et al.</em> 2022</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Animals</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Major intrinsic protein (MIP or Aqp0) of <em>Bos taurus</em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.8.8.20"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.8.8.20&tcname=">1.A.8.8.20</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC>Channel protein </TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC>Cyanobacteria </TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC>Copper homeostasis protein (SmpX) of <I>Synechococcus sp.</i> </TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.8.8.21"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.8.8.21&tcname=">1.A.8.8.21</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Aquaporin x5 of 273 aas and 6 TMSs, Aqp-x5. BIB conductance shows voltage- and dose-dependent block by extracellular divalent cations Ca<sup>2+</sup> and Ba<sup>2+</sup> but not by Mg<sup>2+</sup> in wild-type channels (<span id="ref34553932.14990474" class="cite">Yanochko and Yool 2004</span>). Site-directed mutagenesis of negatively charged glutamate (Glu<sub>274</sub>) and aspartate (Asp<sub>253</sub>) residues had no effect on divalent cation block, but mutation of Glu<sub>71</sub> in the first TMS altered channel properties (<span id="ref34553932.14990474" class="cite">Yanochko and Yool 2004</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Animals</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Big brain (BIB) of <em>Drosophila melanogaster</em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.8.8.30"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.8.8.30&tcname=">1.A.8.8.30</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Aquaporin-A, AqpA of 249 aas and 6 TMSs.
